|     | \*\*Swami Vivekananda\*\*                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                       | \*"This world is the great Târaka Mâyâ; it is a great illusion..."\* / \*"Religion is not in doctrines... it is being and becoming, it is realisation."\* / \*"The more we learn...how ignorant we are..."\* / \*"Until you have that thirst, that desire \[for God\], you cannot get religion... you are no better than any atheist..."\* / \*"Live for an ideal, and leave no place in the mind for anything else."\* / \*"Cast off all fear..."\*2025-06-06 Friday | Worldly (World as \*Māyā\*/Illusion / Ignorance as Default Human State / Critique of Doctrinaire Religion) & Transcendent (Realisation of Atman/Brahman / Intense Thirst for God / Renunciation of Finite Self for Infinite Self) | Vivekananda presents the \*\*Universal Nihilistic Event as the encounter with \*Māyā\*—the profound realization that the empirical world and the ego-self are fundamentally illusory, a "great hypnotic spell" veiling the sole reality of Atman/Brahman.\*\* This is not mere philosophical skepticism but an experiential deconstruction of perceived reality, revealing its ultimate groundlessness relative to the Absolute. "Ignorance" of this truth is the default human condition, and doctrinaire religion often perpetuates illusion rather than leading to "realisation." The "thirst for God" must become an all-consuming, desperate passion (like a drowning man gasping for air) that annihilates all other finite desires and attachments. This radical renunciation of the lesser (illusory) self and world for the "ideal" (the Infinite Self) is the core transformative act. The Void is \*Māyā\* itself, and the "atheist" is the one who remains content within it, lacking the terrible, sacred thirst. | \*\*Ignorance (\*Māyā\* as Negativity):\*\* Interpreted as a purely world-negating, life-denying doctrine of illusion, rather than a complex philosophical concept pointing to the \*relative and dependent\* nature of phenomenal reality compared to an ultimate, unconditioned Ground. The dynamism and creative power often associated with \*Māyā\* in some Vedantic schools is overlooked. \<br\> \*\*Ignorance (Realisation as Mere Belief):\*\* His emphasis on "realisation" is often reduced to intellectual understanding or adoption of Vedantic doctrines, rather than the direct, transformative, ego-shattering experiential encounter with Brahman that he and his tradition advocate. The "thirst" is psychologized as mere religious fervor. | \*\*Displacement (Vedanta as System):\*\* Advaita Vedanta, as systematized, can become another set of doctrines and philosophical arguments used to "explain" and thus contain the direct experience of \*Māyā\* and the terror/liberation of realizing Brahman. The path to "realisation" becomes a structured, often intellectualized, pursuit within a school of thought, potentially buffering the raw encounter. \<br\> \*\*Displacement (Renunciation as Ethical Ideal):\*\* The radical call for renunciation is often softened into an ethical ideal of detachment, selfless service (Karma Yoga \*without\* Jnana Yoga's radical ungrounding), or moderate spiritual discipline, rather than the total ontological shift of identification from the finite ego to the Infinite Atman, which requires a dying to the world as ultimate reality. The comfort of \*Māyā\* is not fully abandoned. |     |
|     | \*\*Extended Densification Cycle 2/3 (Swami Vivekananda):\*\* \<br\> \*\*Nihiltheistic Affinity:\*\* 9/10. Vivekananda’s Vedantic framework involves a radical deconstruction of the phenomenal world as \*Māyā\* (illusion/void relative to Brahman) and demands total renunciation of the ego for realization of the attributeless Absolute. This is a profound nihilism of the finite in service of an ultimate, non-dual Reality. \<br\> \*\*Paradoxical Tension:\*\* The world is an illusion, yet it is the field for realizing the Real. We are already Brahman, yet we must strive through ignorance to realize it. Intense passion (thirst for God) is needed to transcend passion. God is impersonal yet worshipped through forms. \<br\> \*\*Cross-Link (Advaita Vedanta/Shankara):\*\* Vivekananda is a primary modern global exponent of Shankara's Advaita, emphasizing its core tenets of Brahman's sole reality, \*Māyā\*, and the identity of Atman and Brahman, presented with a dynamic, often rationalistic appeal suited to Western audiences. \<br\> \*\*Cross-Link (Meister Eckhart/Pseudo-Dionysius):\*\* Strong parallels in the apophatic description of the Ultimate (Brahman as \*Nirguna\* / Godhead as Nothingness) and the necessity of self-emptying/transcending intellectual concepts to achieve union or realization. Both traditions point to a Reality beyond all finite predication, approached through radical negation. |                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                       |                                                                                                                                                                                                                                   | |     | \*\*Martin Heidegger\*\*                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                      | \*"Anxiety reveals the Nothing."\* (\*What is Metaphysics?\*) / \*"Being-towards-death...is the possibility of the impossibility of existence..."\* (\*Being and Time\*) / \*"Dread reveals Nothing. We are suspended in dread..."\* / \*"Why are there beings at all, and not rather Nothing?"\* (Fundamental Question of Metaphysics) | Worldly (Phenomenological Encounter with Nothingness via Anxiety / Finitude as Ontological Structure / Groundlessness of Beings) & Transcendent (Nothingness as Condition for Revealing Being / Authenticity as Resolute Confrontation with Void) | Heidegger’s nihilistic event is the \*\*primordial phenomenological encounter with "the Nothing" (\*das Nichts\*) through the mood of "anxiety" (\*Angst\*).\*\* This is not fear of a specific object, but an "uncanny" dread wherein the world of beings as a whole "slips away," losing its everyday significance and revealing its ultimate groundlessness. "The Nothing" is not mere privation but that which \*makes manifest\* beings as beings, their astonishing "thatness" against a backdrop of potential non-existence. Being-towards-death further radicalizes this, as Dasein (human existence) confronts its "ownmost possibility of the impossibility of existence"—an encounter with its finite, null basis. The fundamental question "Why beings and not rather Nothing?" directly posits the Void as the ultimate ontological mystery from which Being itself must be wrested or understood. | \*\*Ignorance (The Nothing as Mere Negation):\*\* "The Nothing" is often misunderstood as simply logical negation or the absence of beings, rather than Heidegger’s more radical ontological concept: a positive, albeit "nihilating," phenomenon that is co-disclosed with Being and is essential for Being's revelation. \<br\> \*\*Ignorance (Anxiety as Psychological):\*\* \*Angst\* is frequently reduced to psychological anxiety or existential angst in a purely subjective sense, missing its function in \*Being and Time\* as a \*fundamental attunement\* (\*Stimmung\*) that \*discloses ontological structures\*—specifically, Dasein's thrownness, its Being-in-the-world as care, and its Being-towards-death. \<br\> \*\*Ignorance (Being-towards-death as Morbid):\*\* Interpreted as a morbid preoccupation with death or a call to despair, rather than as the key to "authentic existence"—the resolute acceptance of finitude that liberates Dasein from the illusions of the "they-self" and opens it to its ownmost possibilities. | \*\*Displacement (Existentialism as Humanism):\*\* Heidegger's early work is often assimilated into secular existential humanism (e.g., Sartre, though complexly), where concepts like authenticity and freedom are framed as human self-creation \*within\* a meaningless void, thereby displacing the more mysterious, ontological dimension of "the Nothing" and its relation to the question of Being itself. The Void is presupposed as background, not explored as phenomenon. \<br\> \*\*Displacement (Later Heidegger as Mysticism/Obscurantism):\*\* His later "turn" (\*Kehre\*) towards a more poetic, meditative thinking about Being, language, and technology, often invoking a kind of "waiting" or "releasement" (\*Gelassenheit\*), is sometimes dismissed as obscurantist mysticism or a retreat from philosophy, failing to see it as a continued engagement with the limits of metaphysical language when trying to think the Ground/Abyss that is neither simply Being nor Nothing. The "solution" becomes dismissing the later thought. |     |
|     | \*\*Extended Densification Cycle 2/3 (Martin Heidegger):\*\* \<br\> \*\*Nihiltheistic Affinity:\*\* 10/10. Heidegger places the encounter with "Nothingness" at the very heart of metaphysical inquiry and authentic existence. His entire project can be read as a profound meditation on the groundlessness of beings and the mystery of Being as it emerges from/against this Nothing. \<br\> \*\*Paradoxical Tension:\*\* The Nothing "nihilates" yet allows Being to appear. Anxiety (unease before the Nothing) is the condition for authenticity (freedom). Being-towards-death (finitude) is the key to a whole life. Language speaks Being, but Being withdraws from direct predication. \<br\> \*\*Cross-Link (Kierkegaard/Tillich):\*\* Deeply influenced by Kierkegaard's analysis of Angst and existence. Tillich, in turn, used Heideggerian ontology (especially Nonbeing) to reformulate theological concepts. All three explore anxiety/dread as fundamental to confronting ultimate reality. \<br\> \*\*Cross-Link (Taoism/Zen Buddhism \- especially later Heidegger):\*\* His later concepts of \*Gelassenheit\* (releasement), "letting-be," and thinking as a receptive dwelling resonate strongly with Taoist \*wu wei\* (non-action) and Zen notions of emptiness (\*Śūnyatā\*) and direct, non-conceptual insight. Both point towards a truth beyond the grasp of willful, calculative reason, a truth found in a kind of receptive voiding of the manipulative ego. |                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                         |                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                   |                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                 |
